Months later, I saw a YouTube short showing someone blow up a blaze canister with a fire arrow, and had the realization, I'd gone through the whole game without realizing the depths of the combat and the weakness mechanic. After that, I redownloaded it, and proceeded to do an ultra hard run, and had an absolute blast, loved every second of my time.  Just got HFW, and I can only say wow. All my little gripes are gone. I'm going through Horizon Zero Dawn for the time right now on PC and...   I have decided to turn off the minimap and quest markers and avoid buying the map icons that tell you where everything is.   And play it on very hard.  I am so very pleased with those decisions.  I'm extremely impressed at how this game allows for that kind of gameplay without feeling repetitive, stretched and bland.  The gameplay cycle of exploring, finding exciting things, getting challenged by enemies or needing to gather materials, while needing to stay aware of your surroundings to actually understand where you are and also because machines can actually be dangerous... very nice. Yes, very nice.  Beautifully balanced experience, rewarding, a little challenging, in a superb environment and with a coherent and well-delivered narrative and world.  I had very low hopes for this game, even considering all the good reviews. I don't particularly like dinosaurs or tribal themes and I am allergic to content sprinkled sandboxes.  But with the very hard difficulty, plus no map, icons or handholding at all, this game has a hidden experience that sadly most people probably won't ever play.  I'm truly glad I gave it a shot.

27:22 SPOLIERS FOR HFW I think HFW does answer the question of how tech will make humans better or worse.  The immediate example are the Far Zeniths. They find a way to make them essentially immortal and that leads to exasperating their worst characteristics. It is also interesting when you see Aloy and some of her companions refuse or disgusted by the option to be come immortal ourselves.  It is almost to say that there are some tech we should reject unless we should become worst.  Seeing the Far Zenith living for as long as they did made me wonder how that longevity would impact someone's mental health. Overall, I think HFW says that technology can not improve the human condition alone.  The Far Zeniths find a new planet to live, a way to become immortal, and created a new high tech society and they still repeated the same mistakes (and in fact become more evil) and created their own nemesis. Ted Faro created a bunker where he could live the rest of his days as a playboy dictator despite him being the reason the world was ending as they knew it. Instead, he sought to become immortal which ultimately led to his own demise.  The Quen tribe find technology that most others have not, and yet they still create a oppressive society where they gatekeep information.  On the other hand, Aloy uses technology to make the world better.  In fact, the greatest improvements we see in Aloy does not come from technology, but from her companions who teach her she is not and does need to be alone. However, all these things are subtle and maybe that makes for bad storytelling especially if people do not see them.

There are a few things that I think this review misses. I'm so tired of the Assassin's Creed model of just put a face on screen for all the dialog. In Horizon Forbidden West, there is custom mocap sequences with actors and actresses on-set performing all these dialog sequences. You are watching a play or scene take place in this world, right where they are standing, and it feels natural and not gamey at all. That *really* helps these side quests. It's amazing how the mundane takes on completely new meaning when you can feel the depth of a character. One that really stuck out to me from the early part of the game, was an old man gasping for breath. The detail on his face was absolutely incredible, and I could feel every pained breath as he delivered his lines. These don't feel like fetch quests, even though many are, because they are so character-driven.   The second was in the story. I agree with you that there is a certain formula here that is repeated, from the first game. A very long intro sequence, where the world doesn't open up to you for close to 10 hours. There are some differences this time around. The first game's opening 10 hours, were Aloy finding her place in this world, and discovering a growing threat. She was ostracized and an outsider. It feels very much like the opening to any disaster movie where no one believes the scientist until it's too late. In this game's opening, that threat is already here, Aloy knows exactly who she is, and the destination of where this is going is very clearly telegraphed in a way that makes you want to dig deeper. The first 10 hours of this game, blew by, where the first game required some breaks to slog through it. It's even worse the second playthrough on the first game.  The last point I want to make, is that this is a purely PS5 review. This game is entirely different to experience on a PS5. So much so, that I think it bares a warning for the PS4 version of the game. Yes, it's mostly just resolution, which gamers don't tend to care about. However, they are rarely given a world this dense and with this much detail. In the same ways that you lambast the YouTube compression, and 1440p nature of this video; playing this on PS4 does not present the same experience. I'm sure Digital Foundary has some great content around this, but at the same time I think it's worth addressing that we finally got a game where all of the tricks (raytracing, resolution scaling, draw distance) are all coming together in a package where I'm not really looking for the imperfections. It just runs at a great framerate and looks fantastic. It's a game that will make you forget to ask if it's running in native 4K.
The game is perfect. My only critique is that I wish they make Bandit camps more interesting or lessen them at least. It's my least favourite side quests.

As of the big early June update (patch 1.14 and hotfix 1.15), the Performance mode has been *significantly* upgraded - Guerilla pulled some kind of crazy technical witchcraft and revamped how it worked, such that to my eyes on a PS5 with Samsung 4k TV, Performance mode is now pretty much visually indistinguishable from Resolution mode in terms of detail and clarity. Gone are the midground shimmering artifacts on foliage and other objects - everything is so much clearer and sharper. Guerilla have also stated that they're working on adding support for variable refresh rate screens as well. Beyond that, the pop-in problems have been in large part negated (especially with larger objects and structures), though it's still somewhat noticeable with smaller things like mass foliage. The overall technical performance of the game on PS5 is still not 100% perfect, but not by much. It's still very stable and now considerably better than it was at and just after launch.  In addition, several big new features have been added for free, including New Game + mode and new associated gear to get, Ultra Hard Difficulty, and a fantastic universal armor transmog system. The game is even better now than it was at launch. If you've been waiting, now's your time to jump in!
For those watching in the future, they added a setting to skip the animation when collecting items.

I'm not sure I'd call this game "superb". At least not so far. I mean, it's a game very much built on the assumption that "more is MORE". Compared with the comparatively lean original, if anything, I'd say that Forbidden West feels extremely bloated. Constipated, even. There are too many game mechanics to remember; too much stuff on the weapon wheel. Endless upgrades, customizations and cosmetic shit. And to make matters worse, there's also a shit-ton of combos to memorize. I mean, what the fuck is this? Street Fighter? In the original, you had a light spear attack and a strong spear attack. And then you could care about causing elemental damage if you wanted to, but for the most part, leveling up and bashing machines to hell did the trick. You didn't have to think strategically. You could, and things were quite simple if you did, but you could just as easily button mash and still survive whatever the game threw at you. Now, whenever you run into a foul beast on the prairie, you have a billion options to choose from. Too many weapons, too many subcategories of weapons, too many different arrows and too many passive and active skills to possibly keep any of it together.  That's NOT fun. Not unless you're a toddler on a sugar rush. Nor is it fun that the PS4 version (yeah, good luck finding a PS5) looks absolutely horrendously awful. Granted, I did just recently replay the original game on PC, so I suppose I was somewhat spoiled by that experience, but still, the motion blur and framerate issues on the PS4 version is worse than I've seen in any other game. Pan the camera around and you may as well be under water.  Aloy also has a deeper voice now, which is strange, given how this game picks up right after the first one. And she also has more of an attitude. Not that I mind that as such, but even in the prologue, she throws out a "Shit!!!" a couple of times, and she never did that in the first game. Has she become a more cynical person in 5 minutes or could it be that 5 years have made her writers forget what kind of character she is?  I also hate how question marks appear on the map, only if you're close enough to them. And sometimes, you have to be on the same level as them as well. If you're in a valley, you won't see the question mark up on the mountain before you climb that mountain. Even with all the HUD features turned on. WTF? Just show me where there's stuff to see and I'll go there. I don't have time to spend years in the wild.  What else? Too many machines. Too many things to scan. And Machine Strike wants to be Gwent so badly, but it fucking sucks. Sigh. Things might improve, I suppose, but so far, this is the Red Dead Redemption 2 of the franchise, and that's a very depressing thing to have to say...

The game and its mechanics look splendid; clearly the devs have put much effort. But if I may nitpick on the review a bit, overlooking the issues of the game and giving it a 9.5 is not objective enough. If we deduct just half a point for each of near-field pop-in (really bad, that), story being a weak point, and grind through crafting, it scores lesser. I think we should not lose focus while reviewing it, even if personally we have liked it much. The review content has covered all good and bad points, but I think the decision to try giving it a score at the outset was not prudent.  As for how they managed to make such a masterpiece with a given number of people, it is due to their experience of making HZD, in addition to contemporary hardware/ game engine evolution. It is the ecosystem which makes possible such great games, when the devs want to do so with full support from management like those at Guerrilla Games.

21:20 - Fun fact: They actually pulled the entire concept of the game out of a hat. - Seriously. One day, they let all the employees at the studio write down their ideas, which they'd then throw together to draw one from, and it was like "dinosaur robots in a post-apocalyptic world" or something, and they were like "Sure, let's do it.". - It just goes to show that you don't need to be so calculated when it comes to creativity. You just need to let it flow and let the ideas come, then find some structure and fine-tune it later. - I do this all the frickin' time in things like music and writing. - But companies like Ubisoft are way too afraid to let go of previously successful formulas. And while their creative studios try to do as much with it as possible, they're just told to do it a certain way, preventing the products from reaching new heights, resulting in people criticizing them for being bland. - If only Ubisoft could take the stance of "OK, studios, you do your thing and we'll market it.". Which, for some studios they do, like Massive Entertainment seems like their Motive (EA's studio, which also seems to get more freedom). But then the other studios that do their flagship products, like "Assassin's Creed" and "Far Cry", they seem to get pushed into a (slightly flexible) mold, and that's a damn shame.

lol "Force Mono Audio"... I mean, yea... There's some value to that?... I work in audio, but... I'm still struggling to come up with something. Maybe if you really, REALLY want/need to play this game over one speaker or if you have loss of hearing (I don't know if that makes sense then, but perhaps there's a reasoning there). - But games like this... They should definitely be experienced with glorious audio, at least a good set of stereo speakers or headphones, or better yet, a surround setup of at least 5.1. - Force to mono... That's a peculiar setting.
Pop-in on an SSD... hmm... There's something seriously wrong with how they've programmed the engine to respond then. Something is definitely backed up, making the loading of architecture and other objects have to wait, which is ironic considering the technology. They probably have some limitations in place to maintain performance, rather than to bog it down with everything wanting to load in immediately. So it seems they keep things waiting before loading in. But it's not acceptable if large structures don't appear until you're very near, it just ruins the experience. And yes, if there's anything I hate in videogame-experiences, it's pop-in. I've hated it since any 3D-games in the '90s, so I'm not just gonna change my mind after two decades, especially considering that it shouldn't happen anymore with the technology we have available right in our homes, as well as all kinds of trickery you can do to prevent it. Besides, this game doesn't seem that special in terms of how much there is to be rendered in the world compared to other games in the last 5 years or more.

And I honestly don't really see another way of making the crafting system with in the weapon ammo realm better. If you were to remove the crafting entirely, you'd have to have a system similar to COD or Halo, i.e. picking up ammo drops from enemies or the world. The first could work, but you'd stumble on the same issue as before, you'd spend 10 minutes looting corpses after every fight which is no different to how it currently is, and the second would just be a little contrived depending on locale, like it wouldn't make a whole lot of sense to have random ammo drops just sitting around in the open world. like someone would've taxed them long before you'd ever get them. The crafting in that regard makes total sense within the world and the Aloy's character because she is a hunter, most of the tribes she visits are based around hunting and gathering and that way the ammo collection is connected to the world and the combat at the same time!  I'm not going to argue the looting point as whole though, especially in the OG game, that was tedious as fuck, especially when it came to hunting specific components for new weapons and the health gathering. but again, I don't really know how else you could do it without breaking that tangible blend between the mechanics the world/character. Pop-ins detract from a game, as do any and all flaws. Alone, any single flaw shouldn't warrant hate for a game, but the same goes the other way around; a game having fun gameplay or beautiful art shouldn't be used as an excuse for the glaring flaws that pop in front of you regularly. Too often we see reviewers, fanboys, or just regular players in general up in arms to be defensive about a game, saying stuff like "I like the game, therefore if you think a flaw is a flaw then you're just a hater".  The reality is this is a $70 game, unless you jump through hoops to get it for the original $60 on ps4 with the promised free upgrade which they tried to renege on, and in current year we expect games to not have any issues. And while it's fine now cause it's launch, we should expect them to fix it in future patches. If you bought a car and you love it except for that funny rattling noise it makes, you expect the manufacturer to patch it up for you. Likewise for computers, or any other product really. If you bought a $50 jacket and turns out one of the seams was stitched poorly, but otherwise you love it, you'd still expect a replacement or some fix. How the fix goes about happening varies from product to product, and in some cases it's not practical... but for games, it shouldn't end there and the only way they will keep working on it and adding improvements is when people talk about the flaws and ask for improvements.

The Surfshark ad is extremely misleading. Realistically, every website you're going to be buying from will be using SSL encryption, so every time you send them your card data (once every few years?), that data is encrypted on the way from your PC to the server. Adding a VPN on top is just another layer of encryption. The real danger is the website itself getting hacked, especially if they store your data in plain text, which seems to be depressingly common based on news reports.  Here's an analogy: Imagine photocopying your card, putting it in a safe, then putting the safe inside another safe, going to the grocery store, taking the photocopy out of both safes and handing it to the cashier, who gets to keep it. You have no idea what the cashier is gonna do with it, how they're gonna store it, etc.  If you're worried about hackers stealing your card info, use prepaid cards or debit cards connected to checking accounts that have very little money in them.
Fuck Surshark, Sony itself should have sponsored you for this video.

I've played Horizon Zero Dawn last year so game is pretty fresh in my head and I gotta say that combat is not "the best" in this game. I wouldn't even say it is that good. The fact that you can be attacked anywhere at anytime really inders the discovery side of things. Unlike Monster Hunter, the lack on a lock-on makes it complicated to melee the enemy, and melee is a LOT better than trying to shot something(s) that never stops moving. The absent mini-radar and proper telegraphs (despite the sound signals) just aren't enought to cope fighting multiple enemies at the same time, sometimes with great variety, and changing weapons is not intuitive or easy. After finishing the review I feel a bit confused because there a lot of moments where you go off on a negative aspect of the game and then curb by saying that part is still good or this one aspect makes up for it. Some of these negatives seem pretty important like melee being lackluster, guided climbing, and that Cauldrons aren't really involved with the main story.  With melee it was not too amazing in the first game and from what you showed they still didn't put a big focus on it and locked moves behind skill eventhough its one of the only ways to do non-ranged combat. Then you said Cauldrons do not play into the main story and in the first game they were a pretty big focus, even the dlc for the first game was focused on one. The bigest negative is the guided climbing as they kept it from the first game. Climbing looks like its a fairly big aspect of the game and if you travel on foot climbing is going to be second to the amount of time your running. Climbing is a very large part of open world games as its part of the exploration aspect. Many would hope that the sequel would try to play with climbing as guided climbing as your whole climbing system does not work for open world games. Guided climbing is usually in puzzle or level design that makes it obvious where to go. The only game that really makes it work as climbing isn't a focus is God of War 4 as the guided climbing is used as a way if limiting the camera and doing certain shots for cutscenes in the game. In all truth they should of got help from the Death Stranding Devs who did a climbing system in same engine as they also helped them with their game. Collabrative developing should be encouraged in these cases as it seems like the only company that really gets multiple collabrations is nintendo, its dev teams, and a good amount of studios that make games for the switch. The biggest example would be how BoTW devs got help from Monolith (Xeno Devs) in creating its world. I really enjoyed Horizon Zero Dawn but it feels like they didn't address a good amount of negatives with the first game in its sequel. I hope more people talk about the negatives so the devs know what to improve for the next game because it feels like they didn't hear about these same problems that were in the first game from reviews they looked at.

I was really invested in this review, especially when you spoke about combat and I was really immersed, you said "you know you can see this" but horizon forbidden west is different.  However, you fail to mention how this sequel doesn't have a need or goal or requirement for higher difficulty (no trophy, no reward, no need) to get better and choose a harder mode.  The first game did, with its "complete the game on hard" / new game + and ultra.  But this game doesn't and I have noticed this downward trend in Sony games.   It essentially makes everything you said about combat and its comparisons to monster hunter mute.  Because in monster hunter you are all on a level playing field, no one can choose "easy" and get the same rewards, gratification as someone who had chosen "medium" or "hard".  Monster Hunter also provides the chance later for higher difficulty modes, once you are comfortable.  This progression or need to challenge one self is completely void in this game.   For me personally, any game that gives difficulty options but doesn't provide a reason for them gets an immediate 0/10 (frustrated sarcasm).     Hence why I loved the first game.  I am all for inclusivity, and difficulty curves, but It is a real shame when a game includes so much skill based gameplay and knowledge, but then over the long terms doesn't require the player to use that knowledge for greater reward.   Its nice that Sony provides a comfortable ¬£70 experience, but its a shame they continue to neglect people who actually want to play a challenging video game.  I don't just set a game to hard for the fun of it, the modern world is to fast paced and we are all busy, so if there is nothing for me in it for raising the bar, then why should I ? which makes me arrive at the conclusion that I might as well play on "easy" and that if I have too I might as well not play at all.  A game like horizon would probably be served best with how it decided to do it in the first game, or no difficulty at all.

As good as the music is, I can't help but wonder what the composer tried to do here. I've heard short melodies/themes from multiple other franchises in the background music (Elder Scrolls, Concerning Hobbits from LOTR, Game of Thrones) and they seem to either be hidden references or the composer is kinda lazy (unlikely), but they are to obvious for a composer to be overlooked. This seems intentional, wich is fine if it's just a reference, but I hope there's not too much of that in the game. At least for me, it takes me out of the experience by reminding me of other franchises, instead of keeping me in the game with it's own music. Not the biggest deal and certainly not a dealbreaker, but I'd love to hear what the composer thought.
For those of you that want the best experience, play without crosshairs. It totally transforms the first game and I imagine it'll do the same here. *optional reading ahead if youre intrested*  It adds game-long value to hunting critters. Having to shoot Boars and Rabbits along the way not just for their resources but to get your shots accurate for when you take on machines.   It adds depth into ammo management since you miss a TON of arrows before your good enough to conserve them properly.   It forces you to melee more because you cant be blowing 10 arrows trying to hit a Watchers eyeball.  It forces you to use your full kit because adding status effects requires multipul arrows in a set amount of time so just throwing a couple bombs is often more consistent ‚Äî but theyre expensive.   It also makes 1 shotting machines with the freezing mechanic actually challenging since the freezing mechanic broke the first game. Okay just for reference, how does this game stack up if you're someone who didn't really care for the first game. The big thing for me was how closely it adhered to the Ubisoft formula, where my eye was not guided by landmarks and personal curiosity but rather waypoints that I robotically followed on the map. That also includes better animations and voice acting, since I really loved the premise of the world and story but I was constantly pulled out by subpar delivery and janky automated animations. I'd also like to know if there's a less convoluted inventory UI, since I was often discouraged by looking into my pouch to upgrade my shit and being completely overwhelmed by stats and weapons and materials that I had no idea how important they were or even what they did. Basically, I really wanted to love Zero Dawn, but just couldn't, and I want to know if Forbidden West can pull me in on its own merits or if I should just leave it be.
But will it win GOTY 2022 over Outer Wilds?

I own a PS5, and when I saw the Tweet/YT notification that said "ABSOLUTELY SUPERB" I was like "oh hell yeah, I'll play a gorgeous DECIMA engine sci-fi post-post-apocalypse PS5 game!" Then I started the review, and immediately could feel something was off. Keep in mind, I've been a big ShillUp fan since the Division 2 pivot-era. That means I've literally watched every single review on this channel, even for games I have zero interest in, or games on the Switch or PC (RN I only play Series X & PS5). I think of all the "YouTube Gaming Critics" or whatever, Ralph has not only been pretty damn close to perfect with his takes on games (DEATHLOOP notwithstanding), but he consistently evaluates games with a genuine, unbiased, objective viewpoint‚Äî praising what we as gamers view as "objectively good" qualities: impressive visuals / sound & other technical attributes (AKA "Digital Foundry Stuff"), innovative design, consistently engaging gameplay (especially in terms of variety), innovative design that focuses on challenging the player, but also making sure they're equipped for said challenge (think Elden Ring), a satisfying narrative that's either uniquely gripping (Undertale) or traditionally well-constructed (Uncharted 4). Most importantly I think, is a game's ability to be memorable in some way; small indie games can be just as mind-blowing (Outer Wilds) as bigger ones (Psychonauts 2), but for different reasons. So what the fuck am I getting at? This Review is filled with contradictions to the point where *crowbcat* could make a video showing all of them back to back, and there's a lot. The whole thing has as much praise thrown at Guerilla ‚Äì mainly for visual fidelity / art design, varied combat encounters/ quests, and impressive character moments involving interwoven dialogue ‚Äì as it does _excuses_ for disastrous issues involving *ALL* of those same things. The frame rate is a buttery smooth 60fps, except when it isn't and you have to hard reset. The companions add... something, but the story is entirely drivel and requires _extensive_ knowledge of the previous game, and even then it's still not worth absorbing. The game looks absolutely beautiful, but there are *ENTIRE, MASSIVE STRUCTURES* that _totally disappear until you stand right in front of them, and they pop into view. The combat is the "best of any open world game ever" (absolutely NOT, also totally subjective), but the melee is even _worse_ than the last game, and the traversal is wonky, and the weapons / encounter design relies too heavily on targeting specific elemental weaknesses or whatever. He states multiple times that "This really *sucks."* The traversal, the looting, the LOD pop-in, heavy-handed story, the mid-fight crafting, the ability tree which was only mentioned bc the melee is bad, and so much else I'm literally going to run out of space. No one will read this anyway, but here's my point. The Review starts with "I don't give scores but this is a 9.5 or a 10." *NOWHERE* in this Review (for a game whose predecessor I OWN, and bounced off of after I got Persona 5 & Death Stranding) did I see or hear anything to convince me that this game is not only worth $70, but the cost of a FIVE HUNDRED DOLLAR PS5 _on its own,_ and will "go down in history books as one of the greats." I'm not saying anything other than "you did not make a convincing argument in any way shape of form that this is in ANY way some kind of "instant classic must play", unless you really really like shooting arrows and roll dodging. There's more to the game than that, I'm aware, but none of it looked like it was some kind of *evolution of the open world formula,* because you just said "it's like Witcher 3 or Mass Effect 2, but the enemies are HUGE SPASTIC TRANSFORMERS, and that somehow makes it better." As far as the best combat ever is concerned, after playing the Elden Ring CNT (I'm not even that big on Souls games), I'm calling this *RIGHT NOW* so mark my words‚Äî you will be giving that title to Elden Ring when you review it. The depth, build complexity and just crazy fun addictive combat in the CNT alone makes me sure of that. Idk where your marketing exec "Sony only makes gold" phrases scattered around this video came from, but they seem very misleading for a game with so many problems, and so little real value. I'll comment on the DualSense controller. I have always hated vibration. And DualSense did nothing to change that opinion. For me it's unpleasant and distracting. I can see what they're doing there, but these vibrations still feel so FAKE to me. More than anything, it breaks my immersions because having it enabled is like having "Bzzzz! You have a controller in your hands! Brrr! You're playing a game, not exploring a cave! Bzzzrr!!!!" Every 5 seconds. Which is just annoying. Not to mention that it makes my fingers feel itchy. So yeah, I hate the "Haptic Feedback". Just to tell people - if you disliked vibration before, chances are you won't change your mind. The adaptive triggers though are Super Awesome!... until you realize that adaptive triggers come with a mandatory controller vibration to "enchance your experience" that you can't turn off. You can't have adaptive triggers without the controller vibrating at those moments. Period. This is such a stupid decision, made clearly by someone who couldn't even fathom the idea that someone might dislike Haptic Feedback. Anyway, these are my thoughts. I'm loving the ideas, but I hate the implementation.
I actually really disagree on the real time crafting. It'd be different if you had to pause the entire game to do it, but you can do it while the fight continues. I really enjoy that terrifying feeling of running out of ammo and trying to dodge enemy attacks in slow mo while feverishly crafting more. I think it brings another level of challenge/complexity to the fights that I really like.
